      "content": "Below is a structured summary and key insights from the Citation Lookup and Verification API documentation you’ve provided. This API helps identify, verify, and normalize legal citations within blocks of text or from specified volume-reporter-page inputs, leveraging the Eyecite tool for parsing.\n\n---\n\n### Purpose and Context\n\nThe Citation Lookup API is designed to help users detect and verify legal citations from textual content. It can find citations embedded in text or accept explicit volume, reporter, and page parameters. The API aims to reduce “hallucinated citations” by checking citations against CourtListener’s extensive database.\n\n**Key Capabilities**:  \n- Identify citations in a text blob.\n- Normalize non-standard citation formats or reporter abbreviations.\n- Return corresponding case cluster data, if available.\n- Handle ambiguous citations by returning multiple matches.\n- Throttle requests to prevent abuse and manage performance.\n\n### Endpoints and Usage\n\n**Endpoint**: `/api/rest/v4/citation-lookup/`\n\n**Authentication**: Requires an `Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN` header, like other CourtListener APIs.\n\n**Input Options**:\n\n1. **Text Mode**:  \n   Send a `POST` request with a `text` parameter containing a block of text. The API scans the entire text for potential citations.  \n   Example:  \n   ```bash\n   curl -X POST \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/citation-lookup/\" \\\n     --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n     --data 'text=Obergefell v. Hodges (576 U.S. 644) ...'\n   ```\n\n2. **Structured Mode (Volume-Reporter-Page)**:  \n   Send a `POST` request with `volume=`, `reporter=`, and `page=` parameters.  \n   Example:  \n   ```bash\n   curl -X POST \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/citation-lookup/\" \\\n     --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n     --data 'volume=576' \\\n     --data 'reporter=U.S.' \\\n     --data 'page=644'\n   ```\n\n**Outputs**:  \nThe API returns a JSON list of citation objects. Each object can have:\n\n- **citation**: The original citation parsed.\n- **normalized_citations**: Array of normalized forms. If a non-canonical reporter or typos were present, corrected versions appear here.\n- **start_index & end_index**: Location of the citation in the given text.\n- **status**: HTTP-like codes indicating success or issues:\n  - `200`: Citation found and matched.\n  - `404`: Citation format recognized but no matching case found.\n  - `400`: Invalid or unknown reporter/format.\n  - `300`: Multiple possible matches found (ambiguous citation).\n  - `429`: Too many citations requested in one call, or request throttled.\n- **error_message**: Additional error details when `status` is not `200`.\n- **clusters**: Zero, one, or multiple cluster objects representing cases that match the citation. A “cluster” is typically a grouping of opinions/decisions for that citation.\n\n### Key Behaviors and Examples\n\n1. **Valid Citation Found**:\n   Returns `status: 200`, normalized citation, and cluster data if available.\n\n2. **Citation Not Found**:\n   If the reporter and format are valid but no match exists, returns `status: 404` and an error message.\n\n3. **Too Many Citations in One Request**:\n   If more than 250 citations are included, citations beyond 250 return `status: 429`, though they are still identified and indexed.\n\n4. **Ambiguous Citations**:\n   If a citation can refer to multiple reporters or cases, `status: 300` is returned, and multiple normalized forms and clusters are shown.\n\n5. **Normalization and Typos**:\n   If the input uses a non-canonical abbreviation (like “US” instead of “U.S.”), `normalized_citations` show the corrected form, and if found, returns `status: 200`.\n\n6. **Throttling and Limits**:\n   - Only 60 valid lookups per minute allowed.\n   - Maximum 250 citations per request (additional citations get `429` status).\n   - Maximum of 64,000 characters in `text` per request.\n\n### Limitations\n\n- Does not handle statutes, law journals, “id.” or “supra” references.\n- Does not match citations missing volume or page numbers.\n- If you need advanced matches (like statutes or law journal articles), consider using Eyecite directly.\n- High-volume usage may require careful batching or timing requests to avoid throttling.\n\n### Practical Use Cases\n\n- **Document Verification**:  \n  Run a legal document (e.g., a brief, memo, or opinion draft) through the API to ensure all case citations are real and match correct case law.\n- **Research Tools**:  \n  Integrate with research platforms to highlight, confirm, and link citations on the fly, reducing time spent manually verifying references.\n- **Quality Assurance for Automated Systems**:  \n  Prevent hallucinated citations in AI-generated legal summaries by validating citations before presenting them.\n\n### Additional Resources\n\n- **Eyecite Project**: The underlying technology for citation parsing, developed with the Harvard Library Innovation Lab.\n- **CourtListener Documentation**: Additional details on request formats, cluster object fields, and related endpoints.\n- **Support and Donations**: Consider supporting Free Law Project to maintain and improve these data services.\n\n---\n\n**In Summary**:  \nThe Citation Lookup and Verification API is a powerful tool for validating legal citations, normalizing citation formats, and identifying ambiguous references. By integrating this API into legal research or content generation workflows, users can ensure the accuracy and credibility of referenced case law.",

To learn more about alerts generally, read the alert documentation.\n\nLearn About Alerts\n\nSearch Alerts — /api/rest/v4/alerts/\n\nSearch Alerts update you when there is new information in our search engine.\n\nThis system scales to support thousands or even millions of alerts, allowing organizations to stay updated about numerous topics. This is a powerful system when used with webhooks.\n\nSearch alerts have three required fields:\n\n\n\n\n\nname — A human-friendly name for the alert.\n\n\n\nquery — Search parameters you get from the front end, as a string.\n\n\n\nrate — How frequently you want to receive email updates. Webhook events are always sent in real time. This field accepts the following values:\n\n\n\n\n\nrt — Real time\n\n\n\ndly — Daily\n\n\n\nwly — Weekly\n\n\n\nmly — Monthly\n\nTo learn more about this API, make an HTTP OPTIONS request:\n\ncurl -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/alerts/\" \n\nExample Usage\n\nLet's say we want to know about case law involving Apple Inc. On the front end, we search for \"Apple Inc\" (in quotes) and get query parameters like:\n\nq=%22Apple%20Inc%22&type=o\n\nWe can create that as an alert with an HTTP POST request:\n\ncurl -X POST \\\n  --data 'name=Apple' \\\n  --data 'query=q=%22Apple%20Inc%22&type=o' \\\n  --data 'rate=rt' \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/alerts/\"\n\nThe response:\n\n{\n  \"resource_uri\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/alerts/4839/\",\n  \"id\": 4839,\n  \"date_created\": \"2024-05-02T15:29:32.048912-07:00\",\n  \"date_modified\": \"2024-05-02T15:29:32.048929-07:00\",\n  \"date_last_hit\": null,\n  \"name\": \"Apple\",\n  \"query\": \"q=\\\"Apple Inc\\\"\",\n  \"rate\": \"rt\",\n  \"alert_type\": \"o\",\n  \"secret_key\": \"ybSBXwtDcMKI2SxPZDCEx02DSSUF7EEvx1CjOk4f\"\n}\n\nSearch Alerts can be modified with HTTP PATCH requests. Use this API to create, modify, list, and delete Docket Alerts.\n\nDocket Alerts are always sent as soon as an update is available. See the help page on Docket Alerts to learn more about how we get updates.\n\nDocket Alerts have two fields you can set:\n\n\n\n\n\ndocket — Required: The docket you want to subscribe to or unsubscribe from.\n\n\n\nalert_type — Whether to subscribe or unsubscribe from the docket.\n\nThis field is part of @recap.email's auto-subscribe feature. If you are not using @recap.email or have auto-subscribe disabled, you can ignore this field.\n\nIf you are using @recap.email and have auto-subscribe enabled in your profile, Docket Alerts will be automatically created for you as CourtListener receives notifications about cases. To permanently unsubscribe from a case for which you are receiving notifications from PACER, create an \"Unsubscription\" for the case.\n\nTo learn more about this API, make an HTTP OPTIONS request:\n\ncurl -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9'\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/docket-alerts/\"\n\nExample Usage\n\nTo create       "content": "Below is a structured summary and key insights from the Legal Alert API documentation. This API enables users to create, manage, and delete alerts for new legal information as it becomes available on CourtListener, either through search results or docket updates.\n\n---\n\n### Purpose and Context\n\nThe Legal Alert APIs let you stay updated on legal information relevant to your interests. There are two main types of alerts:\n\n1. **Search Alerts**: Notify you when new documents or cases matching specified search queries become available.\n2. **Docket Alerts**: Notify you when there are new docket entries, documents, or updates for specific cases (dockets).\n\nAlerts can send notifications via email or trigger a webhook event on your server, making it easy to integrate with custom workflows or applications.\n\n### Search Alerts — `/api/rest/v4/alerts/`\n\n**Use Cases**:  \n- Track case law for specific keywords, topics, or parties.\n- Keep updated on newly published opinions or orders that match your interest.\n\n**Key Fields**:  \n- **name**: A human-readable label for the alert.\n- **query**: The search parameters as a URL-encoded string, often obtained from the CourtListener frontend’s query string.\n- **rate**: How frequently email updates should be sent. Possible values:\n  - `rt` (Real Time)\n  - `dly` (Daily)\n  - `wly` (Weekly)\n  - `mly` (Monthly)\n\n*Webhook events* are always real-time, regardless of the `rate` value.\n\n**Example**:  \nCreate an alert for the query `\"Apple Inc\"` that notifies in real time:\n```bash\ncurl -X POST \\\n  --data 'name=Apple' \\\n  --data 'query=q=%22Apple%20Inc%22&type=o' \\\n  --data 'rate=rt' \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/alerts/\"\n```\n\n**Modifying Alerts**:  \nUse `PATCH` to update fields like `rate`, or `DELETE` to remove the alert altogether.\n\n**Listing Alerts**:  \nSend a `GET` request to `/api/rest/v4/alerts/` to list all your current search alerts.\n\n---\n\n### Docket Alerts — `/api/rest/v4/docket-alerts/`\n\n**Use Cases**:  \n- Stay updated on any new filings or docket events in a particular case.\n- Integrate with PACER updates via CourtListener’s RECAP data.\n\n**Key Fields**:  \n- **docket** (required): The docket ID you want alerts for.\n- **alert_type**:  \n  - `1` indicates a subscription (you want updates).\n  - `0` indicates an unsubscription (no updates).\n\n**Example**:  \nCreate a Docket Alert for docket ID `1`:\n```bash\ncurl -X POST \\\n  --data 'docket=1' \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/docket-alerts/\"\n```\n\n**Modifying Alerts**:  \n- Use `PATCH` to change `alert_type` from subscription to unsubscription if you use @recap.email auto-subscribe features.\n- Use `DELETE` to stop receiving alerts for that docket.\n\n**Listing Docket Alerts**:  \nSend a `GET` request to `/api/rest/v4/docket-alerts/` to see all current docket alerts.\n\n---\n\n### Integration and Automation\n\n- **Email Updates**: Receive periodic or real-time notifications right in your inbox.\n- **Webhooks**: Set a webhook URL in your account settings. Whenever there’s an update that matches your alerts, CourtListener will send a POST request to your server with details, enabling immediate, automated processing.\n- **Scalability**: The system supports large numbers of alerts, making it suitable for organizations with broad monitoring needs.\n\n### Coming Soon\n\nA scalable alert system is under development to notify users when certain keywords appear in PACER documents. This feature will further enhance monitoring capabilities.\n\n### Support and Contributions\n\nCourtListener and these APIs are maintained by the Free Law Project, a non-profit. Users are encouraged to support the organization to ensure ongoing development, data curation, and maintenance.\n\n---\n\n**In Summary**:  \nThe Legal Alert APIs provide powerful, flexible tools to monitor case law and docket activity. By setting up search alerts, you can track topics, keywords, or parties across newly published case law. With docket alerts, you remain updated on new filings or changes in ongoing cases. The system’s webhook support, combined with flexible rates for updates, ensures you can keep your workflow efficient, timely, and well-informed.",

      "content": "Below is a structured summary and key insights from the Supreme Court Visualization API documentation you provided. This API allows users to programmatically create, access, and manage network visualizations of U.S. Supreme Court opinion clusters, showing how cases cite one another.\n\n---\n\n### Purpose and Context\n\nThe Supreme Court Visualization API is designed to help users explore the relationships and citation networks between Supreme Court cases (opinion clusters). By providing a start and end case, the API attempts to create a network (a visualization) illustrating the citation paths connecting them. These visualizations can be used for legal research, educational purposes, or analytical studies on case law and its development over time.\n\n### Key Endpoint\n\n**Endpoint**: `/api/rest/v4/visualizations/`\n\n**Authentication**: Requires a valid API token in the request header.\n\n**Data Types**:  \n- **Visualization**: Each visualization corresponds to a graph of interconnected Supreme Court cases.\n- **Clusters**: Represent individual decisions or collections of related decisions. Linked clusters form a citation network.\n\n### Creating a Visualization\n\n**Required Fields**:  \n- **cluster_start**: A URL referencing the starting cluster (e.g., `/api/rest/v4/clusters/105659/`).\n- **cluster_end**: A URL referencing the ending cluster (e.g., `/api/rest/v4/clusters/111891/`).\n- **title**: A human-readable title for the visualization.\n\n**Example**:  \n```bash\ncurl -X POST \\\n  --data 'cluster_start=/api/rest/v4/clusters/105659/' \\\n  --data 'cluster_end=/api/rest/v4/clusters/111891/' \\\n  --data 'title=A map from Trop to Salerno' \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/visualizations/\"\n```\n\n**Outcome**:  \n- If the network between the start and end clusters can be generated and isn’t too large, a visualization is created.\n- The response includes nested JSON data representing the visualization and the clusters involved.\n\n### Managing Visualizations\n\n**Retrieving Visualizations**:  \n- Use `GET` requests to `/visualizations/` or `/visualizations/{id}/` to retrieve visualizations.  \n- You can see public visualizations and any private ones you created.\n\n**Updating (Editing) Visualizations**:  \n- Use `PATCH` to modify fields like `published` (to make it public) or `title`.\n- `PATCH` Example:\n  ```bash\n  curl -X PATCH \\\n    --data 'published=True' \\\n    --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n    \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/visualizations/1/\"\n  ```\n\n**Deleting Visualizations**:\n- To soft-delete, set `deleted=True` using `PATCH`.\n- To hard-delete, send an HTTP `DELETE` request:\n  ```bash\n  curl -X DELETE \\\n    --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n    \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/visualizations/1/\"\n  ```\n\n### Privacy and Sharing\n\n- By default, visualizations are private, visible only to their creator.\n- Setting `published=True` makes them publicly accessible.\n\n### Use Cases\n\n- **Academic Research**: Map out landmark Supreme Court cases and how they influence later decisions.\n- **Legal Analysis**: Understand the citation network to see how certain rulings have shaped legal precedent.\n- **Education**: Visual aids for students studying constitutional law or the evolution of Supreme Court jurisprudence.\n\n### Support and Contributions\n\nThe Supreme Court Visualization API is maintained by the Free Law Project, a non-profit. Users are encouraged to support the initiative and help ensure these open legal data resources remain available and continue to improve.\n\n---\n\n**In Summary**:  \nThe Supreme Court Visualization API provides a powerful tool to visualize the citation pathways connecting Supreme Court cases. By specifying start and end cases, users can generate network diagrams, edit and publish them, and integrate these visualizations into their research or educational materials. This API complements the case law and citation APIs, offering a visual dimension to legal data analysis.",

      "content": "SEARCH API\n\n\n\n\n\nMY TOKEN--- 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9 ------\n\n\n\n\nOverview\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nBasic Usage\n\n\n\nUnderstanding\n\n\n\n\n\nResult Types\n\n\n\nOrdering\n\n\n\nFiltering\n\n\n\nFields\n\n\n\nHighlighting\n\n\n\nResult Counts\n\n\n\nSpecial Notes\n\n\n\nMonitoring a Query\n\nLegal Search API\n\n/api/rest/v4/search/\n\nUse this API to search case law, PACER data, judges, and oral argument audio recordings.\n\nTo get the most out of this API, see our coverage and advanced operators documentation.\n\nBasic Usage\n\nThis API uses the same GET parameters as the front end of the website. To use this API, place a search query on the front end of the website. That will give you a URL like:\n\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/q=foo\n\nTo make this into an API request, copy the GET parameters from this URL to the API endpoint, creating a request like:\n\ncurl -X GET \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token <27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9>' \\\n  'https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/search/?q=foo'\n\nThat returns:\n\n{\n  \"count\": 2343,\n    \"next\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/search/?cursor=cz0yMzUuODcxMjUmcz04MDUzNTUzJnQ9byZkPTIwMjQtMDktMTY%3D&q=foo\",\n    \"previous\": null,\n    \"results\": [\n        {\n            \"absolute_url\": \"/opinion/6613686/foo-v-foo/\",\n            \"attorney\": \"\",\n            \"caseName\": \"Foo v. Foo\",\n            \"caseNameFull\": \"Foo v. Foo\",\n            \"citation\": [\n                \"101 Haw. 235\",\n                \"65 P.3d 182\"\n            ],\n            \"citeCount\": 0,\n            \"cluster_id\": 6613686,\n            \"court\": \"Hawaii Intermediate Court of Appeals\",\n            \"court_citation_string\": \"Haw. App.\",\n            \"court_id\": \"hawapp\",\n            \"dateArgued\": null,\n            \"dateFiled\": \"2003-01-10\",\n            \"dateReargued\": null,\n            \"dateReargumentDenied\": null,\n            \"docketNumber\": \"24158\",\n            \"docket_id\": 63544014,\n            \"judge\": \"\",\n            \"lexisCite\": \"\",\n            \"meta\": {\n                \"timestamp\": \"2024-06-22T10:26:35.320787Z\",\n                \"date_created\": \"2022-06-26T23:24:18.926040Z\",\n                \"score\": {\n                    \"bm25\": 2.1369965\n                }\n            },\n            \"neutralCite\": \"\",\n            \"non_participating_judge_ids\": [],\n            \"opinions\": [\n                {\n                    \"author_id\": null,\n                    \"cites\": [],\n                    \"download_url\": null,\n                    \"id\": 6489975,\n                    \"joined_by_ids\": [],\n                    \"local_path\": null,\n                    \"meta\": {\n                        \"timestamp\": \"2024-06-24T21:14:41.408206Z\",\n                        \"date_created\": \"2022-06-26T23:24:18.931912Z\"\n                    },\n                    \"per_curiam\": false,\n                    \"sha1\": \"\",\n                    \"snippet\": \"\\nAffirmed in part, reversed in part, vacated and remanded\\n\",\n                    \"type\": \"lead-opinion\"\n                }\n            ],\n            \"panel_ids\": [],\n            \"panel_names\": [],\n            \"posture\": \"\",\n            \"procedural_history\": \"\",\n            \"scdb_id\": \"\",\n            \"sibling_ids\": [\n                6489975\n            ],\n            \"source\": \"U\",\n            \"status\": \"Published\",\n            \"suitNature\": \"\",\n            \"syllabus\": \"\"\n        },\n    ...\n\nThat's the simple version. Read on to learn the rest.\n\nUnderstanding the API\n\nUnlike most APIs on CourtListener, this API is powered by our search engine, not our database.\n\nThis means that it does not use the same approach to ordering, filtering, or field definitions as our other APIs, and sending an HTTP OPTIONS request won't be useful.\n\nSetting the Result type\n\nThe most important parameter in this API is type. This parameter sets the type of object you are querying:\n\nTypeDefinitionoCase law opinionsrList of Federal cases (dockets) with up to three nested documents. If there are more than three matching documents, the more_docs field for the docket result will be true.rdFederal filing documents from PACERdFederal cases (dockets) from PACERpJudgesoaOral argument audio files\n\nFor example, this query searches case law:\n\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/q=foo&type=o\n\nAnd this query searches federal filings in the PACER system:\n\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/q=foo&type=r\n\nIf the type parameter is not provided, the default is to search case law.\n\nOrdering Results\n\nEach search type can be sorted by certain fields. These are available on the front end in the ordering drop down, which sets the order_by parameter.\n\nIf your sorting field has null values, those results will be sorted at the end of the query, regardless of whether you sort in ascending or descending order. For example if you sort by a date that is null for an opinion, that opinion will go at the end of the result set.\n\nFiltering Results\n\nResults can be filtered with the input boxes provided on the front end or by advanced query operators provided to the q parameter.\n\nThe best way to refine your query is to do so on the front end, and then copy the GET parameters to the API.\n\nFields\n\nUnlike most of the fields on CourtListener, many fields on this API are provided in camelCase instead of snake_case. This is to make it easier for users to place queries like:\n\ncaseName:foo\n\nInstead of:\n\ncase_name:foo\n\nAll available fields are listed on the advanced operators help page.\n\nTo understand the meaning of a field, find the object in our regular APIs that it corresponds to, and send an HTTP OPTIONS request to the API.\n\nFor example, the docketNumber field in the search engine corresponds to the docket_number field in the docket API, so an HTTP OPTIONS request to that API returns its definition:\n\ncurl -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token <27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9>' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/\" \\\n  | jq '.actions.POST.docket_number'\n\nAfter filtering through jq, that returns:\n\n{\n  \"type\": \"string\",\n  \"required\": false,\n  \"read_only\": false,\n  \"label\": \"Docket number\",\n  \"help_text\": \"The docket numbers of a case, can be consolidated and quite long. In some instances they are too long to be indexed by postgres and we store the full docket in the correction field on the Opinion Cluster.\"\n}\n\nHighlighting Results\n\nTo enhance performance, results are not highlighted by default. To enable highlighting, include highlight=on in your request.\n\nWhen highlighting is disabled, the first 500 characters of snippet fields are returned for fields o, r, and rd.\n\nResult Counts\n\ntype=d and type=r use cardinality aggregation to compute the result count. This enhances performance, but has an error of ±6% if results are over 2000. We recommend noting this in your interface by saying something like, \"About 10,000 results.\"\n\nSpecial Notes\n\nA few fields deserve special consideration:\n\n\n\n\n\nAs in the front end, when the type is set to return case law, only published results are returned by default. To include unpublished and other statuses, you need to explicitly request them.\n\n\n\nThe snippet field contains the same values as are found on the front end. This uses the HTML5 <mark> element to identify up to five matches in a document.\n\nThis field only responds to arguments provided to the q parameter. If the q parameter is not used, the snippet field will show the first 500 characters of the text field.\n\nThis field only displays Opinion text content.\n\n\n\nThe meta field in main documents contains the score field, which is currently a JSON object that includes the bm25 score used by Elasticsearch to rank results. Additional scores may be introduced in the future.\n\nMonitoring a Query for New Results\n\nTo monitor queries for new results, use the Alert API, which will send emails or webhook events when there are new results.\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nFor example, this uses jq to look at the filters on the docket API:\n\ncurl -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/\" | jq '.filters'\n\nThat returns something like:\n\n{\n  \"id\": {\n    \"type\": \"NumberRangeFilter\",\n    \"lookup_types\": [\n      \"exact\",\n      \"gte\",\n      \"gt\",\n      \"lte\",\n      \"lt\",\n      \"range\"\n    ]\n  },\n...\n\nThis means that you can filter dockets using the ID field, and that you can do exact, greater than or equal, greater than, less than or equal, less than, or range filtering.\n\nYou can use these filters with a double underscore. For example, this gets IDs greater than 500 and less than 1,000:\n\ncurl \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/?id__gt=500&id__lt=1000\" | jq '.count'\n499\n\nIt also allows ranges (inclusive):\n\ncurl \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/?id__range=500,1000\" | jq '.count'\n501\n\nFilters can be combined using multiple GET parameters.\n\nThere are a few special types of filters. The first are Related Filters, which allow you to join filters across APIs. For example, when you are using the docket API, you'll see that it has a filter for the court API:\n\n\"court\": {\n    \"type\": \"RelatedFilter\",\n    \"lookup_types\": \"See available filters for 'Courts'\"\n}\n\nThis means that you can use any of the court filters on the docket API. If you do an OPTIONS request on the court API, you'll see its filters:\n\ncurl -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/courts/\" | jq '.filters'\n\nAgain, one of the filters is the ID field, but it only allows exact values on this API:\n\n\"id\": {\n    \"type\": \"CharFilter\",\n    \"lookup_types\": [\n        \"exact\"\n    ]\n}\n\nPutting this together, here's how you look up dockets for a particular court:\n\ncurl \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/?court=scotus&id__range=500,1000\" | jq '.count'\n36\n\nThis opens up many possibilities. For example, another filter on the Court endpoint is for jurisdictions. To use it, you would use a GET parameter like court__jurisdictions=FD. In this case, the double underscore allows you to join the filter from the other court API to the docket API.\n\nIf you want to join a filter, you could do something like court__full_name__startswith=district. That would return dockets in courts where the court's name starts with \"district\".\n\nRelatedFilters can span many objects. For example, if you want to get all the Supreme Court Opinion objects, you will need to do that with a query such as:\n\ncurl \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cluster__docket__court=scotus\"\n\nThis uses the Opinion API to get Opinions that are part of Opinion Clusters that are on Dockets in the Court with the ID of scotus. To understand this data model better, see the case law API documentation.\n\nTo use date filters, supply dates in ISO-8601 format.\n\nA final trick that can be used with the filters is the exclusion parameter. Any filter can be converted into an exclusion filter by prepending it with an exclamation mark. For example, this returns Dockets from non-Federal Appellate jurisdictions:\n\ncurl \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/?court__jurisdiction!=F\"\n\nYou can see more examples of filters in our automated tests.\n\nOrdering\n\nWith the exception of the search API, you can see which fields can be used for ordering, by looking at the ordering key in an OPTIONS request. For example, the Position endpoint contains this:\n\n\"ordering\": [\n    \"id\",\n    \"date_created\",\n    \"date_modified\",\n    \"date_nominated\",\n    \"date_elected\",\n    \"date_recess_appointment\",\n    \"date_referred_to_judicial_committee\",\n    \"date_judicial_committee_action\",\n    \"date_hearing\",\n    \"date_confirmation\",\n    \"date_start\",\n    \"date_retirement\",\n    \"date_termination\"\n]\n\nThus, you can order using any of these fields in conjunction with the order_by parameter.\n\nDescending order can be done using a minus sign. Multiple fields can be requested using a comma-separated list. This, for example, returns judicial Positions ordered by the most recently modified, then by the most recently elected:\n\ncurl \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/positions/?order_by=-date_modified,-date_elected\"\n\nOrdering by fields with duplicate values is non-deterministic. If you wish to order by such a field, you should provide a second field as a tie-breaker to consistently order results. For example, ordering by date_filed will not return consistent ordering for items that have the same date, but this can be fixed by ordering by date_filed,id. In that case, if two items have the same date_filed value, the tie will be broken by the id field.\n\nCounting\n\nTo retrieve the total number of items matching your query without fetching all the data, you can use the count=on parameter. This is useful for verifying filters and understanding the scope of your query results without incurring the overhead of retrieving full datasets.\n\ncurl \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cited_opinion=32239&count=on\"\n\n{\"count\": 3302}\n\nWhen count=on is specified:\n\n\n\n\n\nThe API returns only the count key with the total number of matching items.\n\n\n\nPagination parameters like cursor are ignored.\n\n\n\nThe response does not include any result data, which can improve performance for large datasets.\n\nIn standard paginated responses, a count key is included with the URL to obtain the total count for your query:\n\ncurl \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cited_opinion=32239\"\n\n{\n  \"count\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cited_opinion=32239&count=on\",\n  \"next\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cited_opinion=32239&cursor=2\",\n  \"previous\": null,\n  \"results\": [\n    // paginated results\n  ]\n}\n\nYou can follow this URL to get the total count of items matching your query.\n\nField Selection\n\nTo save bandwidth and increase serialization performance, fields can be limited by using the fields parameter with a comma-separated list of fields.\n\nFor example, to only receive the educations and date_modified fields from the Judge endpoint you could do so with:\n\ncurl \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/people/?fields=educations,date_modified\"\n{\n  \"educations\": [\n    {\n      \"resource_uri\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/educations/12856/\",\n      \"id\": 12856,\n      \"school\": {\n        \"resource_uri\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/schools/4240/\",\n        \"id\": 4240,\n        \"is_alias_of\": null,\n        \"date_created\": \"2010-06-07T17:00:00-07:00\",\n        \"date_modified\": \"2010-06-07T17:00:00-07:00\",\n        \"name\": \"University of Maine\",\n        \"ein\": 16000769\n      },\n      \"person\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/people/16214/\",\n      \"date_created\": \"2023-03-31T07:15:28.556198-07:00\",\n      \"date_modified\": \"2023-03-31T07:15:28.556222-07:00\",\n      \"degree_level\": \"jd\",\n      \"degree_detail\": \"\",\n      \"degree_year\": 1979\n    }\n  ],\n  \"date_modified\": \"2023-03-31T07:15:28.409594-07:00\"\n},\n...\n\nYou can also exclude fields using fields!=educations,date_modified.\n\nUnfortunately, this cannot be used for nested resources, though there is an open issue tracking this.\n\nPagination\n\nMost APIs can be paginated by using the page GET parameter, but that will be limited to 100 pages of results.\n\nAs of API v4, deep pagination is generally enabled for requests that are ordered by the id, date_modified, or date_created field. When sorting by these fields, the next and previous keys of the response are how you must paginate results, and the page parameter will not work.\n\nSome API endpoints support slightly different fields for deep pagination:\n\n\n\n\n\n/api/rest/v4/recap-fetch/ also supports the date_completed field.\n\n\n\n/api/rest/v4/alerts/ and /api/rest/v4/docket-alerts/ only support the date_created field.\n\nRate Limits\n\nOur APIs allow 5,000 queries per hour to authenticated users. Unauthenticated users are allowed 100 queries per day for experimentation.\n\nTo debug throttling issues:\n\n\n\n\n\nTry browsing the API while logged into the website. If this works and your code fails, it usually means that your token authentication is not configured properly, and your code is getting throttled as an anonymous user, not according to your token.\n\n\n\nReview your recent API usage by looking in your profile, but remember that it will show stats for the browsable API as well.\n\n\n\nReview the authentication/throttling tips in our forum.\n\nIf, after checking the above, you need your rate limit increased, please get in touch so we can help.\n\nPerformance Tips\n\nA few things to consider that may increase your performance:\n\n\n\n\n\nAvoid doing queries like court__id=xyz when you can instead do court=xyz. Doing queries with the extra __id introduces a join that can be expensive.\n\n\n\nIn general, less data is easier and faster to get than more. Could you use a field to filter your result set down to something smaller?\n\n\n\nWhen using the search endpoint, smaller result sets are faster. It isn't always possible to tweak your query to return fewer results, but sometimes it is possible to do a more precise query first, thus making a broader query unnecessary. For example, a search for an individual in their expected jurisdiction will be faster than doing it in the entire corpus. This will benefit from profiling in your use case and application.\n\nAdvanced Field Definitions\n\nPlacing an HTTP OPTIONS request on an API is the best way to learn about its fields, but some fields require further explanation. Click below to learn about these fields.\n\nLearn About Fields\n\n\negal Search API\n\n/api/rest/v4/search/\n\nUse this API to search case law, PACER data, judges, and oral argument audio recordings.\n\nTo get the most out of this API, see our coverage and advanced operators documentation.\n\nBasic Usage\n\nThis API uses the same GET parameters as the front end of the website. To use this API, place a search query on the front end of the website. That will give you a URL like:\n\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/q=foo\n\nTo make this into an API request, copy the GET parameters from this URL to the API endpoint, creating a request like:\n\ncurl -X GET \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token <27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9>' \\\n  'https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/search/?q=foo'\n\nThat returns:\n\n{\n  \"count\": 2343,\n    \"next\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/search/?cursor=cz0yMzUuODcxMjUmcz04MDUzNTUzJnQ9byZkPTIwMjQtMDktMTY%3D&q=foo\",\n    \"previous\": null,\n    \"results\": [\n        {\n            \"absolute_url\": \"/opinion/6613686/foo-v-foo/\",\n            \"attorney\": \"\",\n            \"caseName\": \"Foo v. Read on to learn the rest.\n\nUnderstanding the API\n\nUnlike most APIs on CourtListener, this API is powered by our search engine, not our database.\n\nThis means that it does not use the same approach to ordering, filtering, or field definitions as our other APIs, and sending an HTTP OPTIONS request won't be useful.\n\nSetting the Result type\n\nThe most important parameter in this API is type. This parameter sets the type of object you are querying:\n\nTypeDefinitionoCase law opinionsrList of Federal cases (dockets) with up to three nested documents. If there are more than three matching documents, the more_docs field for the docket result will be true.rdFederal filing documents from PACERdFederal cases (dockets) from PACERpJudgesoaOral argument audio files\n\nFor example, this query searches case law:\n\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/q=foo&type=o\n\nAnd this query searches federal filings in the PACER system:\n\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/q=foo&type=r\n\nIf the type parameter is not provided, the default is to search case law.\n\nOrdering Results\n\nEach search type can be sorted by certain fields. These are available on the front end in the ordering drop down, which sets the order_by parameter.\n\nIf your sorting field has null values, those results will be sorted at the end of the query, regardless of whether you sort in ascending or descending order. For example if you sort by a date that is null for an opinion, that opinion will go at the end of the result set.\n\nFiltering Results\n\nResults can be filtered with the input boxes provided on the front end or by advanced query operators provided to the q parameter.\n\nThe best way to refine your query is to do so on the front end, and then copy the GET parameters to the API.\n\nFields\n\nUnlike most of the fields on CourtListener, many fields on this API are provided in camelCase instead of snake_case. This is to make it easier for users to place queries like:\n\ncaseName:foo\n\nInstead of:\n\ncase_name:foo\n\nAll available fields are listed on the advanced operators help page.\n\nTo understand the meaning of a field, find the object in our regular APIs that it corresponds to, and send an HTTP OPTIONS request to the API.\n\nFor example, the docketNumber field in the search engine corresponds to the docket_number field in the docket API, so an HTTP OPTIONS request to that API returns its definition:\n\ncurl -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token <27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9>' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/\" \\\n  | jq '.actions.POST.docket_number'\n\nAfter filtering through jq, that returns:\n\n{\n  \"type\": \"string\",\n  \"required\": false,\n  \"read_only\": false,\n  \"label\": \"Docket number\",\n  \"help_text\": \"The docket numbers of a case, can be consolidated and quite long. In some instances they are too long to be indexed by postgres and we store the full docket in the correction field on the Opinion Cluster.\"\n}\n\nHighlighting Results\n\nTo enhance performance, results are not highlighted by default. To enable highlighting, include highlight=on in your request.\n\nWhen highlighting is disabled, the first 500 characters of snippet fields are returned for fields o, r, and rd.\n\nResult Counts\n\ntype=d and type=r use cardinality aggregation to compute the result count. This enhances performance, but has an error of ±6% if results are over 2000. We recommend noting this in your interface by saying something like, \"About 10,000 results.\"\n\nSpecial Notes\n\nA few fields deserve special consideration:\n\n\n\n\n\nAs in the front end, when the type is set to return case law, only published results are returned by default. To include unpublished and other statuses, you need to explicitly request them.\n\n\n\nThe snippet field contains the same values as are found on the front end. This uses the HTML5 <mark> element to identify up to five matches in a document.\n\nThis field only responds to arguments provided to the q parameter. If the q parameter is not used, the snippet field will show the first 500 characters of the text field.\n\nThis field only displays Opinion text content.\n\n\n\nThe meta field in main documents contains the score field, which is currently a JSON object that includes the bm25 score used by Elasticsearch to rank results. Additional scores may be introduced in the future.\n\nMonitoring a Query for New Results\n\nTo monitor queries for new results, use the Alert API, which will send emails or webhook events when there are new results.",

      "content": "Below is a structured overview and key insights from the Legal Search API documentation you’ve provided. This API, unlike others on CourtListener, queries an Elasticsearch-based search engine for case law, PACER data, judges, and oral argument audio. It’s designed to replicate the functionality of the CourtListener website’s search, allowing you to programmatically access and filter results.\n\n---\n\n### Endpoint and Authentication\n\n**Endpoint**: `/api/rest/v4/search/`  \n**Authentication**: Requires a valid token in the `Authorization: Token <YOUR_TOKEN>` header.\n\n### Basic Usage\n\n1. **Identify Parameters on the Front End**:  \n   - Start on [courtlistener.com](https://www.courtlistener.com), perform your desired search, and note the GET parameters from the URL.\n   \n2. **Build Your API Request**:  \n   - Take the `q` (query) parameter and other parameters from the front-end URL and append them to your API request.\n   - Example:\n     ```bash\n     curl -X GET \\\n       --header 'Authorization: Token YOUR_TOKEN' \\\n       'https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/search/?q=foo'\n     ```\n   - This returns JSON with `count`, `next`, `previous`, and `results`.\n\n### Search Types (Result Type)\n\n**Key Parameter**: `type`  \nThis parameter controls what you are searching:\n\n- `type=o`: Case law opinions (default if not specified)\n- `type=r`: Federal cases (dockets) with up to three nested documents\n- `type=rd`: Federal filing documents from PACER\n- `type=d`: Federal cases (dockets) from PACER (no nested docs)\n- `type=p`: Judges\n- `type=oa`: Oral argument audio files\n\nFor example, to search case law for \"foo\":\n```\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/search/?q=foo&type=o\n```\n\n### Ordering Results\n\n- Set `order_by` parameter to a field’s name to sort results.\n- Sorting behavior aligns with the website’s sorting options.\n- If sorted by a field that can be null, those items appear at the end.\n\n### Filtering Results\n\n- Filters can be applied either via the `q` parameter (using advanced query syntax) or by using standard GET parameters if the front-end provides them.\n- Best practice: Refine your search on the front end and replicate the resulting query parameters in your API request.\n- Consult the advanced operators and coverage documentation for complex filters (e.g., `caseName:\"Roe v. Wade\"`).\n\n### Fields and Field Names\n\n- Fields in this API often use `camelCase` instead of `snake_case`.\n  - Example: `caseName` instead of `case_name`.\n- A complete list of searchable fields and advanced operators is available in the advanced operators documentation.\n- To understand a field’s meaning, find its corresponding endpoint in the main APIs and issue an HTTP OPTIONS request there for definitions.\n\n### Highlighting Results\n\n- Disabled by default for performance.\n- To enable highlighting of matched terms, include `highlight=on`.\n- Without highlighting, the API returns the first 500 chars of the snippet fields for `o`, `r`, and `rd` types.\n\n### Result Counts\n\n- For `type=d` and `type=r`, the count is approximated using cardinality aggregation. Results over 2,000 may have ±6% error.\n- Indicate approximate counts to end-users when using these types.\n\n### Special Notes\n\n- By default, searching case law returns only published opinions. To include unpublished results, you must specify that in your query.\n- Snippet highlighting displays up to five matches and wraps them in `<mark>` elements.\n- The `meta.score` field can contain ranking scores (like bm25) for relevance.\n\n### Monitoring for New Results\n\n- Use the Alert API to set up alerts for new search results (via email or webhook).\n\n### Performance and Rate Limits\n\n- Authenticated users have 5,000 queries/hour; anonymous users have 100/day.\n- To optimize performance:\n  - Narrow your queries.\n  - Use filtering wisely.\n  - Consider using `fields` parameter to limit returned data.\n  - Avoid unnecessary joins (e.g., prefer `court=xyz` over `court__id=xyz`).\n\n### Pagination\n\n- Normal pagination is limited to 100 pages.\n- For deep pagination, use endpoints that support `cursor`-based pagination (provided in `next` and `previous` keys).\n- When sorted by `id`, `date_created`, or `date_modified` fields (among others), cursor-based pagination must be used.\n\n### Counting and Field Selection\n\n- Add `count=on` to get only the total number of matching items without retrieving any results.\n- Use `fields` parameter to limit returned fields (e.g., `?fields=caseName,dateFiled`), reducing bandwidth and speeding up retrieval.\n- Use `fields!=` to exclude fields.\n\n---\n\n**In Summary**:  \nThe Legal Search API lets you programmatically perform searches similar to the CourtListener website’s front-end. You can specify result types, filters, sorting, and highlighting. The API returns JSON, with `camelCase` field names and supports both shallow and deep pagination. For complex queries, set parameters on the website first, then copy them into your API requests. Use Alerts for monitoring changes in search results over time.",

Therefore, this is not strictly a database of judges, but rather a database of people and the positions they hold.\n\nFor example, William Taft served as president, where he appointed justices, but he was also a Supreme Court justice himself. Therefore, he has a single \"person\" record in the API, he has one position record for his role as president, and another position record for his role as a justice.\n\nThere are a number of \"granularity\" fields for dates. These are used to indicate how granular a corresponding date is. For example, if we know the year somebody died but not the month or day, we would put 2010-01-01 as the date of death, and set the date of death granularity field to %Y.\n\nThis approach means that you can still — mostly — filter and sort by these date fields, but with an awareness that the data may be incomplete.\n\nThe APIs\n\nPeople (Judges and Appointers)\n/api/rest/v4/people/\n\nThis API contains the central \"person\" object. As explained above, people can be judges, appointers, or both.\n\nThis object holds the core metadata about the person, including their biographical data, positions held, educational history, ABA ratings, and political affiliations.\n\nA few notes:\n\n\n\n\n\nPosition objects can be quite large, so they are linked in the person object instead of nested within it.\n\n\n\nIf the is_alias_of field has a value, that means the record represents a nickname for the person referenced in the alias field. Alias records make it possible to find a judge by name, even if they sometimes go by Bob instead of Robert. In our database, this field is a self-join.\n\nIn general, you will only want to work with judges where this field is null, indicating a record that represents a person, not an alias to a person.\n\n\n\nThe race and gender fields are not self-reported and should therefore be considered best guesses. We have done our best to gather these fields from reputable sources, but have also supplied values ourselves when it felt appropriate to do so. Some values may be incorrect.\n\nTo create choices for race, we used the U.S. census and added MENA (it has since been added to the census).\n\n\n\nThe has_photo field indicates whether we have a photo for the judge in our database of judge portraits.\n\n\n\nThe ftm_* fields relate to state court judges, who raise money for elections. Use these fields to link judges to their IDs on Follow The Money, where you can gather and analyze the details.\n\nThese fields have not been updated in many years, but we can do so as a service.\n\nPositions\n/api/rest/v4/positions/\n\nUse this API to learn the positions held by a person, including their time as president, in private practice, as a judge, or in any number of other roles in society or the judiciary.\n\nTo look up field descriptions or options for filtering, ordering, or rendering, complete an HTTP OPTIONS request.\n\nTo filter to positions for a particular person:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/positions/?person=20\"\n\nPolitical Affiliations\n/api/rest/v4/political-affiliations/\n\nUse this API to learn the political affiliations of a person. You don’t necessarily need a special chat model or a multimodal environment in Anaconda to do this. Instead, you can use any programming language or command-line tool to send HTTP requests to the CourtListener API, as long as you include your token and other parameters correctly.\n\n**Here’s how you can get started:**\n\n1. **Set Up Your Environment**:  \n   Since you mentioned Anaconda, you likely have Python available. You can install the Python `requests` library if you don’t have it:\n   ```bash\n   conda install requests\n   ```\n   \n   Or, if you prefer `pip`:\n   ```bash\n   pip install requests\n   ```\n\n2. **Use Python and Requests**:  \n   Below is a basic example of how you can make a GET request to the CourtListener API to search for a query:\n   ```python\n   import requests\n\n   # Your API token\n   token = \"27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9\"\n\n   # Example search parameters\n   params = {\n       'q': 'Apple Inc',  # your query\n       'type': 'o'        # search case law opinions, for example\n   }\n\n   # Make the request\n   response = requests.get(\n       \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/search/\",\n       headers={'Authorization': f'Token {token}'},\n       params=params\n   )\n\n   # Check status and print results\n   if response.status_code == 200:\n       data = response.json()\n       print(\"Count:\", data.get(\"count\"))\n       for result in data.get(\"results\", []):\n           print(result.get(\"caseName\"), result.get(\"absolute_url\"))\n   else:\n       print(\"Error:\", response.status_code, response.text)\n   ```\n\n3. **Adjust Queries as Needed**:  \n   You can modify `params` to include filters, ordering, and other query parameters you learned about from the documentation.       "content": "se law API\n\nCase Law API 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9\n\nUse these APIs to access our huge and growing database of case law.\n\nTo learn more about this collection, including the cases it has and how we get new data each day, see our coverage page on the topic.\n\nThis data is organized into a number of objects. An overview of these objects is described in this section, and greater detail is provided for each, below.\n\nThe four most important objects in this data set are courts, dockets, clusters, and opinions. Together, these hold most of the information from a single case:\n\n\n\n\n\nCourt objects hold information about thousands of courts in this country, including their name, abbreviation, founding date, and more. Every docket is joined to a court to indicate where the case was filed.\n\n\n\nDocket objects hold metadata about the case like the date it was initiated or terminated, the docket number, and more. Every cluster is joined to a docket.\n\n\n\nCluster objects group together opinions when a panel hears a case and there is more than one decision, such as a dissent, concurrence, etc. Clusters are an abstraction we created. Every opinion is joined to a cluster.\n\n\n\nOpinion objects contain the text of the decision and the metadata related to the individual panel member that wrote it.\n\nPutting this all together, dockets are filed in particular courts and contain clusters of opinions.\n\nIf you are looking for a particular piece of metadata, you will find it at the lowest object from the list above where it would not be repeated in the database.\n\nFor example, you could make the docket number a field of the opinion object. This would be fine until you had more than one opinion in a cluster, or more than one cluster joined to a docket. When that happened, you would wind up repeating the docket number value in each opinion object. Instead, if you make it a field of the docket object, you only have to save it to one place: The docket that binds together the clusters and opinions.\n\nAnother example is the opinion text. You could make it a field of the cluster, say, but, again, that wouldn't work, since it wouldn't be clear which opinion the text was a part of in a case with a dissent, concurrence, and majority opinion.\n\nThere are two other objects in the case law database:\n\n\n\n\n\nCitation objects link together which opinion objects cite each other. For more information, see their help page.\n\n\n\nParenthetical objects are extracted from the opinion text when a decision explains a citation it relies on as authority. These are not yet available in an API, but are available as bulk data.\n\nThe APIs\n\nDockets — /api/rest/v4/dockets/\n\nDocket objects sit at the top of the object hierarchy. In our PACER database, dockets link together docket entries, parties, and attorneys.\n\nIn our case law database, dockets sit above Opinion Clusters. In our oral argument database, they sit above Audio objects.\n\nTo look up field descriptions or options for filtering, ordering, or rendering, complete an HTTP OPTIONS request:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/\"\n\nTo look up a particular docket, use its ID:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/4214664/\"\n\nThe response you get will not list the docket entries, parties, or attorneys for the docket (doing so doesn't scale), but will have many other metadata fields:\n\n{\n  \"resource_uri\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/4214664/\",\n  \"id\": 4214664,\n  \"court\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/courts/dcd/\",\n  \"court_id\": \"dcd\",\n  \"original_court_info\": null,\n  \"idb_data\": null,\n  \"clusters\": [],\n  \"audio_files\": [],\n  \"assigned_to\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/people/1124/\",\n  \"referred_to\": null,\n  \"absolute_url\": \"/docket/4214664/national-veterans-legal-services-program-v-united-states/\",\n  \"date_created\": \"2016-08-20T07:25:37.448945-07:00\",\n  \"date_modified\": \"2024-05-20T03:59:23.387426-07:00\",\n  \"source\": 9,\n  \"appeal_from_str\": \"\",\n  \"assigned_to_str\": \"Paul L. Friedman\",\n  \"referred_to_str\": \"\",\n  \"panel_str\": \"\",\n  \"date_last_index\": \"2024-05-20T03:59:23.387429-07:00\",\n  \"date_cert_granted\": null,\n  \"date_cert_denied\": null,\n  \"date_argued\": null,\n  \"date_reargued\": null,\n  \"date_reargument_denied\": null,\n  \"date_filed\": \"2016-04-21\",\n  \"date_terminated\": null,\n  \"date_last_filing\": \"2024-05-15\",\n  \"case_name_short\": \"\",\n  \"case_name\": \"NATIONAL VETERANS LEGAL SERVICES PROGRAM v. United States\",\n  \"case_name_full\": \"\",\n  \"slug\": \"national-veterans-legal-services-program-v-united-states\",\n  \"docket_number\": \"1:16-cv-00745\",\n  \"docket_number_core\": \"1600745\",\n  \"pacer_case_id\": \"178502\",\n  \"cause\": \"28:1346 Tort Claim\",\n  \"nature_of_suit\": \"Other Statutory Actions\",\n  \"jury_demand\": \"None\",\n  \"jurisdiction_type\": \"U.S. Government Defendant\",\n  \"appellate_fee_status\": \"\",\n  \"appellate_case_type_information\": \"\",\n  \"mdl_status\": \"\",\n  \"filepath_ia\": \"https://www.archive.org/download/gov.uscourts.dcd.178502/gov.uscourts.dcd.178502.docket.xml\",\n  \"filepath_ia_json\": \"https://archive.org/download/gov.uscourts.dcd.178502/gov.uscourts.dcd.178502.docket.json\",\n  \"ia_upload_failure_count\": null,\n  \"ia_needs_upload\": true,\n  \"ia_date_first_change\": \"2018-09-30T00:00:00-07:00\",\n  \"date_blocked\": null,\n  \"blocked\": false,\n  \"appeal_from\": null,\n  \"tags\": [\n    \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/tag/1316/\"\n  ],\n  \"panel\": []\n}\n\nThe name of a docket can change in response to the outside world, but the names of clusters do not change. Therefore, we have case_name fields on both the docket and the cluster.\n\nFor example, a suit filed against the EPA administrator might be captioned Petroleum Co. v. Regan. That would go into the case name fields of the docket and any decisions that were issued. But if the administrator resigns before the case is resolved, the docket would get a new case name, Petroleum Co. v. New Administrator, while the case name fields on the clusters would not change.\n\nFor more information on case names, see the help article on this topic.\n\nClusters — /api/rest/v4/clusters/\n\nThis is a major API that provides the millions of Opinion Clusters that are available on CourtListener.\n\nAs with all other APIs, you can look up the field descriptions, filtering, ordering, and rendering options by making an OPTIONS request:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/clusters/\"\n\nA few notes:\n\n\n\n\n\nThe id field of the cluster is used in case law URLs on CourtListener.\n\n\n\nThe sub_opinions field provides a list of the opinions that are linked to each cluster.\n\n\n\nThere are several fields with judge information, such as judges, panel, non_participating_judges, etc. Some of these fields contain strings and others are linked to records in our judge API. When we are able to normalize a judge's name into a record in the judge database, we do so. If not, we store their name in a string field for later normalization.\n\nOpinions — /api/rest/v4/opinions/\n\nThis API contains the text and other metadata about specific decisions.\n\nAs with all other APIs, you can look up the field descriptions, filtering, ordering, and rendering options by making an OPTIONS request:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  -X OPTIONS \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/\"\n\nA few notes:\n\n\n\n\n\nThe type field indicates whether the item is a concurrence, lead opinion, dissent, etc. The values provided for this field are proceeded by numbers so that if they are sorted, they will also be sorted from highest priority to lowest. The most common type of opinion is a \"Combined Opinion\" this is what we label any opinion that either cannot be identified as a specific type, or that contains more than one type.\n\n\n\nThe download_url field contains the original location where we scraped the decision. Many courts do not maintain Cool URIs, so this field is often unreliable.\n\n\n\nThe local_path field contains the path to the binary file for the decision, if we have one. To use it, see the help article on this topic.\n\n\n\nThe opinions_cited field has a list of other opinions cited by the one you are reviewing.\n\n\n\nThe citations field will contain a list of parallel citations for the cluster. See the citation API for details.\n\n\n\nEach opinion has several text fields that will be populated depending on the cluster's source field. For example, scraped opinions tend not to have great text while those from the Harvard corpus do. The best way to get the text for an opinion is to choose the first populated fields from the list below (from best to worst):\n\n\n\n\n\nhtml_with_citations is generated by finding citations in the text of the other fields. All items should eventually have this field, though it can be empty initially or if our citation lookup utility fails. In general, this field is used to generate pages on CourtListener.\n\n\n\nhtml_columbia will be populated if we got the content from the Columbia collaboration.\n\n\n\nhtml_lawbox will be populated if we got the content from the Lawbox donation.\n\n\n\nxml_harvard will be populated if the source was Harvard's Caselaw Access Project. This field has a lot of data but is not always perfect due to being created by OCR instead of by humans.\n\n\n\nhtml_anon_2020 will be populated if we got the content from our anonymous source in 2020.\n\n\n\nhtml will be populated if we got the opinion from a court's website as a Word Perfect or HTML document, or if we got the opinion from Resource.org, which provided HTML documents.\n\n\n\nplain_text will be populated if we got the opinion from a court's website as a PDF or Microsoft Word document.\n\nCourts — /api/rest/v4/courts/\n\nThis API contains data about the courts we have in our database, and is joined into nearly every other API so that you can know where an event happened, a judge worked, etc.\n\nTo look up field descriptions or options for filtering, ordering, or rendering, complete an HTTP OPTIONS request.\n\nYou can generally cache this API. It does not change often.\n\nAPI Examples\n\nFiltering to Opinions in a Court\n\nOpinions are joined to clusters, which join to dockets, and finally to courts. Therefore, one way to get opinions in a specific court is to use a filter like cluster__docket__court=XYZ (note the use of double underscores):\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cluster__docket__court=scotus\"\n\nThat returns:\n\n{\n  \"next\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cluster__docket__court=scotus&cursor=cD0xMDUxNjI5NA%3D%3D\",\n  \"previous\": null,\n  \"results\": [\n      {\n          \"resource_uri\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/9973155/\",\n          \"id\": 9973155,\n  ...\n\nSuch an approach is fine if all you want is the opinion object, but often you'll want the docket and the cluster too.\n\nIn that case, start by getting the dockets with a filter like court=XYZ, then use the IDs in those dockets to get clusters and opinions.\n\nFor example, this gets the dockets from SCOTUS:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/?court=scotus\"\n\nThe first result contains a clusters key like:\n\n\"clusters\": [\n    \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/clusters/9502621/\"\n],\n\nSo we can simply get that URL:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/clusters/9502621/\"\n\nThat returns a cluster, which has the following keys:\n\n\"docket\": \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/68533094/\",\n\"sub_opinions\": [\n    \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/9969234/\"\n],\n\nFinally, GET the links in the sub_opinions field to have the complete object:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/9969234/\"\n\nFiltering by Docket Number\n\nIf you know a docket number, you can use it to look up a docket, cluster, or opinion:\n\nA docket by docket number:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/?docket_number=23A994\"\n\nA cluster by docket number:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/clusters/?docket__docket_number=23A994\"\n\nAn opinion by docket number:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/opinions/?cluster__docket__docket_number=23A994\"\n\nDocket numbers are not unique, so you'll want to add a court filter too:\n\n\n\n\n\nFor dockets, add: &court=scotus\n\n\n\nFor clusters, add: &docket__court=scotus\n\n\n\nFor opinions, add: &cluster__docket__court=scotus\n\nYou may also find the search API helpful, since it will do fuzzy docket searches.\n\nMaking a Custom Case Law Corpus\n\nA common need by empirical researchers is a collection of case law about a particular topic. To build such a corpus, use the search API to identify cases and use these APIs to download them.\n\nFinding a Case by URL\n\nIf you know the URL of a case, you can find it in the cluster API. For example, Obergefell v. Hodges has this URL, with cluster ID 2812209:\n\nhttps://www.courtlistener.com/opinion/2812209/obergefell-v-hodges/\n\nThis case can be found in the cluster API using that same ID:\n\ncurl -v \\\n  --header 'Authorization: Token 27cb3521fc97253116933795c20d3987b11865e9' \\\n  \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/clusters/2812209/\"\n\nOpinion IDs do not reliably match cluster IDs.",

      "content": "Below is a Python example that demonstrates how you might query the CourtListener Case Law API. This code uses your provided token and shows how to:\n\n1. Fetch dockets from a particular court.\n2. Retrieve a cluster and its opinions once you have a docket.\n3. Filter by docket number or other metadata.\n\nReplace `\"YOUR_TOKEN_HERE\"` with your actual token.\n\n```python\nimport requests\n\n# Your API token\ntoken = \"YOUR_TOKEN_HERE\"\n\n# Example 1: Get dockets from a specific court (e.g., the Supreme Court 'scotus')\nparams = {\n    'court': 'scotus'\n}\nresponse = requests.get(\n    \"https://www.courtlistener.com/api/rest/v4/dockets/\",\n    headers={'Authorization': f'Token {token}'},\n    params=params\n)\n\nif response.status_code == 200:\n    data = response.json()\n    print(\"Number of dockets found for SCOTUS:\", data.get(\"count\", \"Unknown\"))\n    results = data.get(\"results\", [])\n    if results:\n        # Take the first docket\n        first_docket = results[0]\n        print(\"First docket:\", first_docket.get(\"case_name\"), first_docket.get(\"docket_number\"))\n\n        # The docket object has a 'clusters' field which contains URLs to cluster objects\n        clusters = first_docket.get(\"clusters\", [])\n        if clusters:\n            cluster_url = clusters[0]\n            # Get the cluster\n            cluster_response = requests.get(\n                cluster_url,\n                headers={'Authorization': f'Token {token}'}\n            )\n            if cluster_response.status_code == 200:\n                cluster_data = cluster_response.json()\n                print(\"Cluster case name:\", cluster_data.get(\"case_name\"))\n                sub_opinions = cluster_data.get(\"sub_opinions\", [])\n                if sub_opinions:\n                    # Fetch an opinion\n                    opinion_url = sub_opinions[0]\n                    opinion_response = requests.get(\n                        opinion_url,\n                        headers={'Authorization': f'Token {token}'}\n                    )\n                    if opinion_response.status_code == 200:\n                        opinion_data = opinion_response.json()\n                        print(\"Opinion ID:\", opinion_data.get(\"id\"))\n                        print(\"Opinion Type:\", opinion_data.get(\"type\"))\n                        # Print a snippet of the text if available\n                        print(\"Opinion Text (excerpt):\", opinion_data.get(\"plain_text\", \"\")[:500])\n                    else:\n                        print(\"Error fetching opinion:\", opinion_response.status_code, opinion_response.text)\n            else:\n                print(\"Error fetching cluster:\", cluster_response.status_code, cluster_response.text)\n        else:\n            print(\"No clusters available for this docket.\")\n    else:\n        print(\"No results returned for SCOTUS dockets.\")\nelse:\n    print(\"Error fetching dockets:\", response.status_code, response.text)\n\n```\n\n**What this code does:**\n\n- Sends a GET request to the `/dockets/` endpoint, filtering by `court=scotus`.\n- Prints the number of dockets found and retrieves the first one.\n- Gets the cluster URL(s) from that docket and fetches the first cluster.\n- From the cluster, it fetches the first opinion (if any), printing basic metadata and a snippet of the opinion text.\n\n**Adjusting Queries:**\n\n- To filter by a specific docket number, you can add `docket_number=YOUR_DOCKET_NUMBER` to `params`.\n- To find opinions from another court, change `court=scotus` to another court ID or remove it to list all available dockets.\n- You can chain filters as described in the documentation, for example:\n  ```python\n  params = {\n      'docket_number': '23A994',\n      'court': 'scotus'\n  }\n  ```\n\n**Additional Notes:**\n\n- If you want to find a specific known case (by cluster ID), you can directly GET the cluster endpoint (e.g., `/api/rest/v4/clusters/2812209/`).\n- If you have a docket number and a court, you can query `clusters` or `opinions` directly by using filters like `?docket__docket_number=...` and `?docket__court=...`.\n- Always check the `count` and `next`/`previous` fields to handle pagination when many results are returned.",

The case involves denied requests for audio recordings, procedural irregularities, and administrative errors. Legal Framework for Court Records The legal framework governing access to court records in Hawaii Family Court cases is complex and multifaceted. At its foundation are the Hawaii Family Court Rules (HFCR), which provide specific guidelines for court procedures and record access. HFCR Rule 80 is particularly relevant, as it governs requests for transcripts and audio/video recordings1. This rule stipulates that written requests must be submitted, stating the reason for the request, and court approval is necessary for confidential proceedings. The Hawaii Court Records Rules (HCRR) further delineate the process for accessing court records. HCRR Rule 10 outlines the procedure for requesting access, while Rule 9 identifies which records are considered confidential and the protocols for accessing them2. These rules work in conjunction with the Hawaii Revised Statutes (HRS), particularly Chapter 92F, known as the Uniform Information Practices Act, which governs public access to government records3. Specific to family court matters, HRS §571-84.6 addresses the confidentiality of Family Court records4. This statute recognizes the sensitive nature of family court proceedings and provides for heightened protection of certain information, especially when it involves minors. Constitutional provisions also play a crucial role in this legal framework. The Due Process Clause of the 14th Amendment to the U.S. Constitution and Article I, Section 5 of the Hawaii State Constitution guarantee due process rights, which can be implicated in issues of court record access5. The Hawaii Supreme Court has emphasized the importance of these rights in family court proceedings, recognizing that denial of access to records could potentially infringe on a party's ability to prepare their case effectively. It's important to note that while the general rule is that most family court records are public, there are significant exceptions. Records involving minors, certain sensitive information, and specific case types (such as adoption) often have heightened confidentiality protections. Judges also have discretion to seal records if deemed necessary to protect privacy interests or the best interests of a child6. When requesting records, parties must navigate this complex framework. The Department of the Attorney General provides specific forms for requesting hearing recordings, typically provided on compact discs (CD-R)7. However, the process can be challenging, especially for pro se litigants, as it requires understanding and compliance with multiple rules and statutes. casetext.com favicon casetext.com favicon oip.hawaii.gov favicon 7 sources Filing a Motion to Reconsider When filing a Motion to Reconsider in the Hawaii Family Court, it's crucial to structure the motion strategically and comprehensively. Begin by citing Hawaii Family Court Rules (HFCR) Rule 59 and Rule 60, which provide grounds for reconsideration1. These rules allow for the correction of manifest errors of law or fact, or the presentation of newly discovered evidence. In the motion, clearly state the case number, hearing dates, and specific records requested. Emphasize the importance of these records for ensuring due process and the ability to present a full and fair case. Reference HFCR Rule 80, which governs requests for transcripts and audio/video recordings1. Address any procedural irregularities observed during previous hearings. For instance, if time limits unduly restricted the ability to present evidence, cite relevant case law such as Doe v. Doe, 134 H. 221, which emphasizes the importance of due process in family court proceedings2. Highlight the best interests of the child standard, as mandated by Hawaii Revised Statutes (HRS) §571-463. Argue that full access to records is necessary to ensure this standard is met and that all decisions are made with complete information. Include a detailed explanation of why the denial of audio records or other court documents impedes your ability to prepare and present your case effectively. Cite the Due Process Clause of the 14th Amendment to the U.S. Constitution and Article I, Section 5 of the Hawaii State Constitution to support your argument for access to these records4. If there have been administrative errors, such as incorrect attorney listings or unexplained denials, request that these be corrected as part of the motion. Provide any supporting documentation or affidavits that substantiate your claims of procedural irregularities or administrative mistakes. Request specific relief, such as access to the denied audio recordings, a new hearing if necessary, or the correction of any errors in the court record. Be clear and concise in stating the exact outcome you are seeking from the court. Finally, ensure that the motion is properly served to all parties involved, following HFCR Rule 5 on service and filing procedures5. Be prepared to argue your motion before the judge, focusing on legal arguments and the importance of the records for your case. Remember, while judges have discretion in family court matters, this discretion must be exercised reasonably and in accordance with the law. By presenting a well-crafted motion that addresses all relevant legal and procedural points, you increase the likelihood of a favorable outcome in your request for reconsideration. casetext.com favicon oaoa.hawaii.gov favicon law.justia.com favicon 5 sources Addressing Privacy Concerns When addressing privacy concerns in Hawaii Family Court cases, it's crucial to balance the need for transparency with the protection of sensitive information. Some common irregularities that may occur include: Failure to allow a party to present relevant evidence or testimony1 Misapplication or incorrect interpretation of laws by the judge1 Bias or prejudice demonstrated by the judge1 Improper consideration or weighing of evidence1 Lack of clear reasoning provided for decisions1 These types of errors can potentially change the outcome of a case and may provide grounds for appeal. For an irregularity to be considered grounds for appeal, it generally needs to be something that could have affected the final decision1. Some specific examples of impactful procedural errors include: Violation of due process rights, such as denying a party the opportunity to be heard1 Making factual findings that clearly contradict the evidence presented1 Failing to consider relevant legal principles that should have been applied1 Decisions that are unreasonable or arbitrary to the point of being an abuse of discretion1 The failure to identify and accommodate cognitive difficulties or other vulnerabilities of parties can also amount to a serious procedural irregularity. In one case, a mother's unidentified cognitive issues led to her inability to properly participate and present evidence, resulting in an unjust outcome that was later appealed2. Strict time limits imposed by judges can sometimes unduly restrict a party's ability to present their case fully. The Hawaii Supreme Court found that a three-hour time limit for a custody trial was an abuse of discretion, as it prevented the court from properly determining the best interests of the children3. Procedural errors can have profound impacts beyond just the legal outcome. They may re-traumatize victims of domestic abuse who are seeking protection through the courts4. The stress and fear associated with navigating an unfair process can exacerbate mental health issues and even lead to tragic outcomes in extreme cases4. It's important to note that not all mistakes or irregularities will necessarily affect the final outcome or provide grounds for appeal. The error typically needs to be significant enough that it could have changed the decision in the case1. Parties who believe procedural irregularities have occurred should consult with an experienced attorney to evaluate the situation and determine the best course of action. justanswer.com favicon researchingreform.net favicon law.justia.com favicon 4 sources Strategies for Pro Se Representation in Family Court When representing yourself in family court, implementing effective strategies can significantly improve your chances of success. Document everything: Keep detailed records of all communications, court appearances, and interactions related to your case. This documentation can be crucial if procedural irregularities occur5.
